21sn (30:8) Recording the message will enable the prophet to use it in the future as evidence that God warned his people of impending judgment and clearly spelled out the nation's guilt. An official record of the message will also serve as proof of the prophet's authority as God's spokesman.
22tn (30:9) Or perhaps, "instruction."
23tn (30:10) Heb "who."
24tn (30:10) Heb "Do not see for us right things."
25tn (30:10) Heb "Tell us smooth things, see deceptive things."
26sn (30:11) The imagery refers to the way or path of truth, as revealed by God to the prophet.
27tn (30:11) Heb "the Holy One of Israel." See the note at 1:4.
28tn (30:12) The sentence actually begins with the word "because." In the Hebrew text vv. 12-13 are one long sentence.
29tn (30:12) Heb "and you trust in oppression and cunning."
30tn (30:12) Heb "and you lean on it."
31tn (30:13) The verse reads literally, "So this sin will become for you like a breach ready to fall, bulging on a high wall, the breaking of which comes suddenly, in a flash." Their sin produces guilt and will result in judgment. Like a wall that collapses their fall will be swift and sudden.
32tn (30:14) Heb "Its shattering is like the shattering of a jug of [i.e., "made by"] potters, [so] shattered one cannot save [any of it]."
33tn (30:14) The words "large enough" are supplied in the translation for clarification.
34tn (30:14) Heb "to remove fire from the place of kindling."
35tn (30:14) On the meaning of abg, "cistern," see HALOT 170.
36tn (30:15) Heb "the Holy One of Israel." See the note at 1:4.
37tn (30:15) Heb "in returning and in quietness you will be delivered."
38tn (30:15) Heb "in quietness and in trust is your strength."
39tn (30:17) Heb "One thousand from before [or, "because of"] one battle cry." hrug is often defined as "threat," but in war contexts it likely refers to a shout or battle cry. See Ps 76:6 [ET].
40tn (30:17) Heb "from before [or, "because of"] the battle cry of five you will flee.
41tn (30:17) Heb "until you are left."
42tn (30:18) Heb "Therefore the LORD waits to show you mercy, and therefore he is exalted to have compassion on you." The logical connection between this verse and what precedes is problematic. The point seems to be that Judah's impending doom does not bring God joy. Rather the prospect of their suffering stirs within him a willingness to show mercy and compassion, if they are willing to seek him on his terms.
43tn (30:18) Heb "Happy are all who wait for him."
44tn (30:19) Heb "For people in Zion will live, in Jerusalem, you will weep no more." The phrase "in Jerusalem" could be taken with what precedes. Some prefer to emend bv@y} , "will live" (a Qal imperfect) to bv@y{ (a Qal active participle) and translate, "For [you] people in Zion, who live in Jerusalem, you will weep no more."
45tn (30:19) Heb "he will indeed show you mercy at the sound of your crying out; when he hears, he will answer you."
46tn (30:20) The Hebrew term translated "sovereign master" here is yn´d)a& (a&d{n´y).
47tn (30:20) Heb "and the Master will give to you bread--distress, and water--oppression."
48tn (30:20) Heb "but your teachers will no longer be hidden, your eyes will be seeing your teachers." The translation assumes that the form iyrwm is a plural participle, referring to spiritual leaders such as prophets and priests. Another possibility is that the form is actually singular (see GKC §93.ss) or a plural of respect, referring to God as the master teacher. See HALOT 560-61. For discussion of the views, see Oswalt, Isaiah, 560.
49tn (30:21) Heb "your ears."
50tn (30:21) The word "correct' is supplied in the translation for clarification.
51tn (30:22) Heb "the platings of your silver idols."
52tn (30:22) Heb "the covering of your gold image."
53tn (30:23) Heb "and he will give rain for your seed which you plant in the ground, and food [will be] the produce of the ground, and it will be rich and abundant."
54tn (30:23) Or, "in that day."
55tn (30:24) Heb "the oxen and the donkeys that work the ground."
56sn (30:24) Crops will be so abundant that even the work animals will eat well.
57tn (30:25) Or, "in the day of."
58sn (30:26) Light here symbolizes restoration of divine blessing and prosperity. The number "seven" is used symbolically to indicate intensity. The exact meaning of the phrase "the light of seven days" is uncertain; it probably means "seven times brighter" (see the parallel line).
59tn (30:26) Heb "the fracture of his people."
sn (30:26) The LORD is here compared to a physician setting a broken bone in a bandage or cast.
60tn (30:26) Heb "the injury of his wound." The joining of synonyms emphasizes the severity of the wound. Another option is to translate, "the wound of his blow." In this case the pronominal suffix might refer to the LORD, not the people, yielding the translation, "the wound which he inflicted."
61sn (30:27) The "name" of the LORD sometimes stands by metonymy for the LORD himself, see Exod 23:21; Lev 24:11; Pss 54:1 (ET, v. 3 HT); 124:8. In Isa 30:27 the point is that he reveals that aspect of his character which his name suggests--he comes as Yahweh ("he is present"), the ever present helper of his people who annihilates their enemies and delivers them. The name "Yahweh" originated in a context where God assured a fearful Moses that he would be with him as he confronted Pharaoh and delivered Israel from slavery in Egypt. See Exod 3.
62tn (30:27) Heb "his anger burns, and heaviness of elevation." The meaning of the phrase "heaviness of elevation" is unclear, for hacm, "elevation," occurs only here. Some understand the term as referring to a cloud (elevated above the earth's surface), in which case one might translate, "and in heavy clouds." Others relate the noun to acm, "burden," and interpret it as a reference to judgment. In this case one might translate, "and with severe judgment." The present translation assumes that the noun refers to his glory and that "heaviness" emphasizes its degree.
63tn (30:27) Heb "his lips are full of anger, and his tongue is like consuming fire." The LORD's lips and tongue are used metonymically for his word (or perhaps his battle cry; see v. 31).
64tn (30:28) Heb "his breath is like a flooding river." This might picture the LORD breathing heavily as he runs down his enemy, but in light of the preceding verse, which mentions his lips and tongue, "breath" probably stands metonymically for the word or battle cry that he expels from his mouth as he shouts. In Isa 34:16 and Ps 33:6 the LORD's "breath" is associated with his command.
65tn (30:28) Heb "shaking nations in a sieve of worthlessness." It is not certain exactly how awv, "emptiness, worthlessness," modifies "sieve." A sieve is used to separate grain from chaff and isolate what is worthless so that it might be discarded. Perhaps the nations are likened to such chaff; God's judgment will sift them out for destruction.
66tn (30:28) Heb "and a bit that leads astray [is] in the jaws of the peoples." Here the nations are likened to horse that can be controlled by a bit placed in its mouth. In this case the LORD uses his sovereign control over the "horse" to lead it to its demise.
67tn (30:29) Heb "[you will have] joy of heart, like one going with a flute to enter the mountain of the LORD to the Rock of Israel."
68tn (30:30) Heb "the LORD will cause the splendor of his voice to be heard."
69tn (30:30) Heb "and reveal the lowering of his arm."
70tn (30:30) Heb "and a flame of consuming fire."
71tn (30:31) Heb "Indeed by the voice of the LORD Assyria will be shattered."
72tc (30:32) The Hebrew text has "every blow from a founded [i.e., "appointed"?] cudgel." The translation above, with support from a few medieval Hebrew MSS, assumes an emendation of hdswm, "founded," to hr)swm, "his discipline."
73tn (30:32) Heb "which the LORD lays on him."
74tn (30:32) Heb "will be with."
75tn (30:32) The Hebrew text reads literally, "and with battles of brandishing [weapons?] he will fight against him." Some prefer to emend twmjlmbw, "and with battles of," to twljmbw, "and with dancing." Note the immediately preceding references to musical instruments.
76tn (30:33) Or, "indeed."
77tc (30:33) The Hebrew text reads literally, "for arranged from before [or, "yesterday"] is [?]." The meaning of htpt, which occurs only here, is unknown. The translation assumes an emendation to tp#T), "Topheth," and places the final he on the beginning of the next word as an interrogative particle. Topheth was a place near Jerusalem used as a burial ground (see Jer 7:32; 19:11).
78tn (30:33) The Hebrew text reads literally, "Also it is made ready for the king, one makes it deep and wide." If one takes the final he on htpt and prefixes it to <g as an interrogative particle (see the preceding note), one can translate, "Is it also made ready for the king?" In this case the question is rhetorical and expects an emphatic affirmative answer, "Of course it is!"
79tn (30:33) Heb "its pile of wood, fire and wood one makes abundant."
sn (30:33) Apparently this alludes to some type of funeral rite.
1tn (31:1) Heb "Woe [to] those who go down to Egypt for help."
2tn (31:1) Heb "and trust in chariots for they are many."
3tn (31:1) Heb "and in horsemen for they are very strong [or, "numerous"]."
4tn (31:1) Heb "the Holy One of Israel." See the note at 1:4.
5sn (31:2) This statement appears to have a sarcastic tone. The royal advisers who are advocating an alliance with Egypt think they are wise, but the LORD possesses wisdom as well and will thwart their efforts.
6tn (31:2) Heb "and he does not turn aside [i.e., "retract"] his words."
7tn (31:2) Heb "and he will arise against the house of the wicked."
8sn (31:2) That is, Egypt.
9tn (31:2) Heb "and against the help of the doers of sin."
10tn (31:3) Heb "will extend."
11tn (31:3) Heb "together all of them will come to an end."
12tn (31:4) Heb "As a lion growls, a young lion over its prey." In the Hebrew text the opening comparison is completed later in the verse ("so the LORD will come down..."), after a parenthesis describing how fearless the lion is. The translation above divides the verse into three sentences for English stylistic reasons.
13tn (31:4) Heb "Though there is summoned against it fullness of shepherds, by their voice it is not terrified, and to their noise it does not respond."
14tn (31:4) Some prefer to translate the phrase lu abxl as "fight against," but the following context pictures the LORD defending, not attacking, Zion.
15tn (31:5) Heb "just as birds fly." The words "over a nest" are supplied in the translation for clarification.
16tn (31:5) The only other occurrence of this verb is in Exod 12:13, 23, 27, where the LORD "passes over" (i.e., "spares") the Israelite households as he comes to judge their Egyptian oppressors. The noun js^P#, "Passover," is derived from the verb. The use of the verb in Isa 31:5 is probably an intentional echo of the Exodus event. As in the days of Moses the LORD will spare his people as he comes to judge their enemies.
17tn (31:6) Heb "Return to the one [against] whom the sons of Israel made deep rebellion." The syntax is awkward here. A preposition is omitted by ellipsis after the verb (see GKC 446, note 2), and there is a shift from direct address (note the second plural imperative "return") to the third person (note "they made deep"). For other examples of abrupt shifts in person in poetic style, see GKC §144.p.
18tn (31:7) Or, "in that day."
19tn (31:7) Heb "reject."
20tn (31:7) Heb "the idols of their idols of silver and their idols of gold which your hands made for yourselves [in] sin." afj, "sin," is understood as an adverbial accusative of manner. See Oswalt, Isaiah, 573, note 4.
21tn (31:8) Heb "Assyria will fall by a sword, not of a man."
22tn (31:8) Heb "and a sword not of humankind will devour him."
23tn (31:8) Heb "he will flee for himself from before a sword."
24tn (31:9) Heb "rocky cliff," viewed metaphorically as a place of defense and security.
25tn (31:9) Heb "His rocky cliff, because of fear, will pass away [i.e., "perish"]."
26tn (31:9) Heb "and they will be afraid of the flag, his officers."
27sn (31:9) The "fire" and "fire pot" here symbolize divine judgment, which is heating up like a fire in Jerusalem, waiting to be used against the Assyrians when they attack the city.
1tn (32:1) Heb "will reign according to fairness."
2tn (32:1) Heb "will rule according to justice."
3tn (32:2) Heb "a man," but vya probably refers here to "each" of the officials mentioned in the previous verse.
4tn (32:3) Heb "Eyes that see."
5tn (32:3) The Hebrew text as vocalized reads literally, "will not gaze," but this is contradictory to the context. The verb form should be revocalized hn´yu#vT=, from uuv, "be blinded" (see Isa 6:10; 29:9).
6tn (32:3) Heb "ears that hear."
7tn (32:4) Heb "the heart of rashness will understand knowledge."
8tn (32:6) Or, "foolishness," in a moral-ethical sense. See 9:17.
9tn (32:6) Heb "and his heart commits sin."
10tn (32:6) Heb "in order to do [or, "so that he does"] what is godless [or, "defiled"]."
11tn (32:6) Heb "so that he leaves empty the appetite [or, "desire"] of the hungry."
12tn (32:6) Heb "and the drink of the thirsty he causes to fail."
13tn (32:7) Heb "as for a deceiver, his implements [or, "weapons"] are evil."
14tn (32:7) Or, "he plans evil things."
15tn (32:7) Heb "to ruin the poor with words of falsehood, even when the needy speak what is just."
16tn (32:8) Heb "and he upon honorable things stands."
17tn (32:9) Or, "self-assured."
18tn (32:9) Or, "self-confident."
19tn (32:10) Heb "days upon a year."
20tn (32:10) Or perhaps, "olive." See 24:13.
21tn (32:11) The imperatival forms in v. 11 are problematic. The first (wdrj, "tremble") is masculine plural in form, though spoken to a feminine plural addressee (twnnav, "complacent ones"). The four imperatival forms that follow (hzgr, "shake with fear;" hfvp, "strip off your clothes;" hru, "expose yourselves;" and hrwgj, "put on") all appear to be lengthened (so-called "emphatic") masculine singular forms, even though they too appear to be spoken to a feminine plural addressee. GKC §48.i suggests emending wdrj to hdrj and understanding all five imperatives as feminine plural "aramaized" forms.
22tc (32:12) The Hebrew text has "over mourning breasts." The reference to "breasts" would make sense in light of v. 11, which refers to the practice of women baring their breasts as a sign of sorrow (see Oswalt, Isaiah, 585). However, one expects the preposition lu to introduce the source or reason for mourning (see vv. 12b-13a) and the participle <ydps, "mourning," seems odd modifying "breasts." The translation above assumes a twofold emendation: (1) <y]d~v*, "breasts," is emended to [<]yd~c*, "field" (a term that also appears in Isa 56:9). The final mem would be enclitic in this case, not a plural indicator. (The Hebrew noun hdc, "field," forms its plural with an to- ending.) (2) The plural participle, <ydps is emended to hd´p)s=, a lengthened imperatival form, meaning "mourn."
23tn (32:13) "Mourn" is supplied in the translation for stylistic reasons. In the Hebrew text vv. 12-13 are one long sentence.
24tn (32:13) Heb "indeed, over all the houses of joy." It is not certain if this refers to individual homes or to places where parties and celebrations were held.
25sn (32:13) This same phrase is used in 22:2.
26tn (32:14) Or, "noisy."
27tn (32:14) Hebrew lpu, probably refers here to a specific area within the city of Jerusalem. See HALOT 861.
28tn (32:14) The Hebrew text has toru*m= du^b=, "??? caves." The force of dub, which usually means "behind, through, round about," or "for the benefit of," is uncertain here. HALOT 616 takes torum as a homonym of "cave" and define it here as "cleared field." Despite these lexical problems, the general point of the statement seems clear--the city will be uninhabited.
29tn (32:14) Heb "the joy of wild donkeys, a pasture for flocks."
30tn (32:15) Heb "until a spirit is emptied out on us from on high." The words "this desolation will continue" are supplied in the translation for clarification and stylistic purposes. The verb hru, used here in the Niphal, normally means "lay bare, expose." The term jwr, "spirit," is often understood here as a reference to the divine spirit (cf. 44:3), but it appears here without an article, pronominal suffix, or a genitive (such as "of the LORD"). The translation assumes that it carries an impersonal nuance "vivacity, vigor" in this context.
31sn (32:15) The same statement appears in 29:17b, where, in conjunction with the preceding line, it appears to picture a reversal. Here it seems to depict supernatural growth. The desert will blossom into an orchard, and the trees of the orchard will multiply and grow tall, becoming a forest.
32sn (32:16) This new era of divine blessing will also include a moral/ethical transformation, as justice and fairness fill the land and replace the social injustice so prevalent in Isaiah's time.
33tn (32:17) Heb "and the product of fairness will be peace."
34tn (32:17) Heb "and the work of fairness [will be] calmness and security forever."
35tn (32:18) Or, "in safe resting places."
36tn (32:19) The Hebrew text reads literally, "and [?] when the forest descends." The form drbw is often understood as an otherwise unattested denominative verb meaning "to hail" (HALOT 154). In this case one might translate, "and it hails when the forest is destroyed." Perhaps the text alludes to a powerful wind and hail storm that knocks down limbs and trees. Some prefer to emend the form to dryw, "and it descends," which provides better, though not perfect, symmetry with the parallel line. Perhaps drbw should be dismissed as dittographic. In this case the statement ("when the forest descends") lacks a finite verb and seems incomplete, but perhaps it is subordinate to v. 20.
37tn (32:19) Heb "and in humiliation the city is laid low."
38tn (32:20) Heb "by all the waters."
39tn (32:20) Heb "who set free the foot of the ox and donkey."
sn (32:20) This verse seems to anticipate a time when fertile land is available to cultivate and crops are so abundant that the farm animals can be allowed to graze freely.
1tn (33:1) Heb "Woe [to] the destroyer."
sn (33:1) In this context "the destroyer" appears to refer collectively to the hostile nations (vv. 3-4). Assyria would probably have been primary in the minds of the prophet and his audience.
2tn (33:1) Heb "and the deceitful one." ywh, "woe [to]," does double duty in the parallel structure.
3tc (33:1) The form in the Hebrew text appears to derive from an otherwise unattested verb hln. The translation follows a 1Qa in reading itlkk, a Piel infinitival form from the verbal root hlk, meaning "finish."
4tn (33:2) The Hebrew text reads literally, "Be their arm each morning." "Arm" is a symbol for strength. The mem suffixed to the noun has been traditionally understood as a third person suffix, but this is contrary to the context, where the people speak of themselves in the first person. The mem is probably enclitic with ellipsis of the pronoun, which can be supplied from the context. See Oswalt, Isaiah, 589, note 1.
5tn (33:2) Heb "[Be] also our deliverance in the time of distress."
6tn (33:3) Heb "at the sound of tumult the nations run away."
7tn (33:3) Heb "because of your exaltation the nations scatter."
8tn (33:4) The pronoun is plural; the statement is addressed to the nations who have stockpiled plunder from their conquests of others.
9tn (33:4) Heb "and your plunder is gathered, the gathering of the locust."
10tn (33:4) Heb "like a swarm of locusts swarming on it."
11tn (33:5) Or, "elevated."
12tn (33:5) Or, "for."
13tn (33:5) Heb "on high."
14tn (33:6) Heb "and he is the stability of your times."
15tn (33:6) Heb "a rich store of deliverance, wisdom, and knowledge."
16tn (33:6) Heb "the fear of the LORD, it is his treasure."
17tn (33:7) The meaning of the Hebrew word is unknown. Proposals include "heroes," "priests," "residents [of Jerusalem]." The translation above assumes that the term is synonymous with "messengers of peace," with which it corresponds in the parallel structure of the verse.
18tn (33:7) Heb "messengers of peace," apparently those responsible for negotiating the agreements that have been broken (see v. 8).
19tn (33:8) Or, "desolate."
20tn (33:8) Heb "the one passing by on the road ceases."
21tn (33:8) Heb "one breaks a treaty."
22tc (33:8) The Hebrew text reads literally, "he despises cities." The term <yru, "cities," is probably a corruption of an original <ydu, "[legal] witnesses," a reading that is preserved in 1Qa. Confusion of dalet and resh is a well attested scribal error.
23tn (33:8) Heb "he does not regard human beings."
24tn (33:9) Or, "earth."
25tn (33:9) Or, "mourns" (BDB 5). HALOT 6-7 lists homonyms, lba I, "mourn," and lba II, "dry up." They propose the second here on the basis of parallelism. See 24:4.
26tn (33:9) Heb "Lebanon is ashamed." The Hiphil is exhibitive, expressing the idea, "exhibits shame." In this context the statement alludes to the withering of vegetation.
27sn (33:9) Sharon was a fertile plain along the Mediterranean coast. See 35:2.
28tn (33:9) Or, "Arabah." See 35:1.
29sn (33:9) Both of these areas were known for their trees and vegetation. See 2:13; 35:2. fertile
30tn (33:9) Heb "shake off [their leaves]."
31tn (33:10) Or, "lift myself up."
32tn (33:11) The second person verb and pronominal forms in this verse are plural. The hostile nations are the addressed, as the next verse makes clear.
33sn (33:11) The hostile nations' plans to destroy God's people will come to nothing; their hostility will end up being self-destructive.
34tn (33:12) Heb "will be a burning to lime." See Amos 2:1.
35tn (33:14) Or, "trembling."
36tn (33:14) Or, "the defiled."
37tn (33:14) The words "they say" are supplied in the translation for clarification.
38tn (33:14) Or, "perpetual, everlasting."
39tn (33:15) Heb "walks."
40tn (33:15) Or, possibly, "justly."
41tn (33:15) Heb "[who] shakes off his hands from grabbing hold of a bribe."
42tn (33:15) Heb "[who] shuts his ear from listening to bloodshed."
43tn (33:15) Heb "[who] closes his eyes from seeing evil."
44tn (33:16) Heb "he [in the] exalted places will live."
45tn (33:16) Heb "mountain strongholds, cliffs [will be] his elevated place."
46tn (33:17) Heb "your eyes will see a king in his beauty."
47tn (33:17) Heb "a land of distances," i.e., an extensive land.
48tn (33:18) Heb "your heart will meditate on terror."
49tn (33:18) The words "and you will ask yourselves" are supplied in the translation for clarification and stylistic reasons.
50sn (33:18) The people refer to various Assyrian officials who were responsible for determining the amount of taxation or tribute Judah must pay to the Assyrian king.
51tn (33:19) The Hebrew form zuwn, is a Niphal participle derived from zuy (an otherwise unattested verb) or from zzu, "be strong" (unattested elsewhere in the Niphal). Some prefer to emend the form to zu@ol, which occurs in Ps 114:1 with the meaning "speak a foreign language." See HALOT 809, 533. In this case, one might translate "people who speak a foreign language."
52tn (33:19) Heb "a people too deep of lip to hear." The phrase "deep of lip" must be an idiom meaning "lips that speak words that are unfathomable [i.e., incomprehensible]."
53tn (33:19) Heb "derision of tongue there is no understanding." The Niphal of gul occurs only here. In the Qal and Hiphil the verb means "to deride, mock." A related noun is used in 28:11.
54tn (33:20) Heb "your eyes."
55tn (33:20) Or, "that does not travel."
56tn (33:21) Heb "But there [as] a mighty one [will be] the LORD for us."
57tn (33:21) Heb "a place of rivers, streams wide of hands [i.e., on both sides]."
58tn (33:21) Heb "a ship of rowing will not go into it."
59tn (33:21) Heb "and a mighty ship will not pass through it."
60tn (33:23) The words "though at this time" are supplied in the translation for clarification. The first half of the verse is addressed to Judah and contrasts the nation's present weakness with its future prosperity. Judah is compared to a ship that is incapable of sailing.
61tn (33:23) Heb "they do not fasten the base of their mast." On /k, "base," see BDB 487, and HALOT 483.
62tn (33:23) Or, perhaps, "flag."
63tn (33:23) Heb "then there will be divided up loot of plunder [in] abundance."
64sn (33:23) Judah's victory over its enemies will be so thorough there will be more than enough plunder for everyone, even slow-moving lame men who would normally get left out in the rush to gather the loot.
65tn (33:24) The words "of Zion" are supplied in the translation for clarification.
1tn (34:1) Heb "the world and its offspring."
2tn (34:3) Heb "will be cast aside."
3tn (34:3) Heb "[as for] their corpses, their stench will arise."
4tn (34:3) Heb "hills will dissolve from their blood."
5tc (34:4) Heb "and all the host of heaven will rot." 1Qa inserts "and the valleys will be split open," but this reading may be influenced by Mic 1:4. On the other hand, the statement, if original, could have been omitted by homoioarcton, a scribe's eye jumping from the conjunction prefixed to "the valleys" to the conjunction prefixed to the verb "rot."
6tn (34:4) Heb "like the withering of a leaf from a vine, and like the withering from a fig tree."
7tn (34:5) The words "he says" are supplied in the translation for clarification. The LORD speaks at this point.
8tn (34:5) Heb "indeed [or, "for"] my sword is drenched in the heavens." 1Qa has hart, "[my sword] appeared [in the heavens]," but this is apparently an attempt to make sense out of a difficult metaphor.
sn (34:5) In v. 4 the "host of the heaven" refers to the heavenly luminaries (stars and planets, see, among others, Deut 4:19; 17:3; 2 Kgs 17:16; 21:3, 5; 23:4-5; 2 Chr 33:3, 5) that populate the divine/heavenly assembly in mythological and pre-scientific Israelite thought (see Job 38:7; Isa 14:13). As in 24:21, they are viewed here as opposing God and being defeated in battle.
9sn (34:5) Edom is mentioned here as epitomizing the hostile nations that oppose God.
10tn (34:6) The verb is a rare Hotpaal passive form. See GKC §54.h.
11tn (34:6) The words "it drips" are supplied in the translation for stylistic reasons.
12tn (34:6) The words "and is covered" are supplied in the translation for stylistic reasons.
13tn (34:6) Heb "for there is a sacrifice to the LORD."
14sn (34:6) The LORD's judgment of Edom is compared to a bloody sacrificial scene.
15tn (34:6) Heb "great."
16tn (34:7) Heb "will go down."
17tn (34:7) Heb "and bulls along with strong ones." Perhaps this refers to the leaders.
18tn (34:8) Heb "for a day of vengeance [is] for the LORD."
19tn (34:8) Heb "a year of repayment for the strife of Zion." The translation assumes that byr refers to Edom's hostility toward Zion. Another option is to understand byr as referring to the LORD's taking up Zion's cause. In this case one might translate, "a time when he will repay Edom and vindicate Zion."
20sn (34:9) That is, Edom.
21tn (34:10) Heb "it will not be extinguished."
22tn (34:11) taq refers to some type of bird (cf. Lev.11:18; Deut 14:17) that was typically found near ruins (see Zeph 2:14). dwpq may also refer to a type of bird, but some have suggested a rodent may be in view.
23tn (34:11) Heb "will possess it."
24tn (34:11) The Hebrew text has bruw [wvny. Both the [wvny (see Lev 11:17; Deut 14:16) and bru (Lev 11:15; Deut 14:14) were types of wild birds.
25tn (34:11) Heb "he"; the referent (the LORD) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
26tn (34:11) Heb "stones," i.e., the stones used in a plumb bob.
27sn (34:11) The metaphor in v. 11b emphasizes that God has carefully planned Edom's demise.
28tn (34:12) Heb "will be nothing."
29tn (34:13) The words "will grow" are supplied in the translation for stylistic reasons.
30tc (34:13) Heb "and she will be a settlement for wild dogs, a dwelling place for ostriches." The translation assumes an emendation of ryx!j*, "grass," to rx@j*, "settlement."
31tn (34:14) Heb "will meet."
32tn (34:14) Heb "and a goat will call to its neighbor."
33tn (34:14) The precise meaning of tylyl is unclear, though in this context the word certainly refers to some type of wild animal or bird. The word appears to be related to hlyl, "night." Some interpret it as the name of a female night demon, on the basis of an apparent Akkadian cognate used as the name of a demon. Later Jewish legends also identified Lilith as a demon.
34tn (34:14) Heb "and will find for themselves a resting place."
35tn (34:15) Hebrew zwpq occurs only here; the precise meaning of the word is uncertain.
36tn (34:15) For this proposed meaning for Hebrew flm, see HALOT 589.
37tn (34:15) Heb "and brood [over them] in her shadow."
38tn (34:15) The precise meaning of hyd is uncertain, though the term appears to refer to some type of bird of prey, perhaps a vulture.
39tn (34:16) Heb "Seek from upon the scroll of the LORD and read."
sn (34:16) It is uncertain what particular scroll is referred to here. Perhaps the phrase simply refers to this prophecy and is an admonition to pay close attention to the details of the message.
40tn (34:16) Heb "one from these will not be missing." hnh, "these," is feminine plural in the Hebrew text. It may refer only to the birds mentioned in v. 15b or may include all of the creatures listed in vv. 14b-15 (all of which are identified with feminine nouns).
41tn (34:16) Heb "each its mate they will not lack."
42tc (34:16) The Hebrew text reads literally, "for a mouth, it has commanded." 1Qa and a few medieval MSS have whyp, "his mouth [has commanded]," while a few other medieval MSS read hwhy yp, "the mouth of the LORD [has commanded]."
43tn (34:16) Heb "and his spirit, he gathers them." The pronominal suffix ("them") is feminine plural, referring to the birds mentioned in v. 15b or to all of the creatures listed in vv. 14b-15 (all of which are identified with feminine nouns).
44tn (34:17) Heb "and he causes the lot to fall for them." Once again the pronominal suffix ("them") is feminine plural, referring to the birds mentioned in v. 15b or to all of the creatures listed in vv. 14b-15 (all of which are identified with feminine nouns).
45tn (34:17) Heb "and his hand divides for them with a measuring line." The pronominal suffix ("them") now switches to masculine plural, referring to all the animals and birds mentioned in vv. 11-15, some of which were identified with masculine nouns. This signals closure for this portion of the speech, which began in v. 11. The following couplet (v. 17b) forms an inclusio with v. 11a through verbal repetition.
46tn (34:17) Heb "will possess it."
1tn (35:1) The final mem on the verb <wccy is dittographic (note the initial mem on the following noun rbdm. The ambiguous verbal form is translated as a jussive because it is parallel to the jussive form lg}t*. The jussive is used rhetorically here, not as a literal command or prayer.
2tn (35:1) Or, "Arabah."
3tn (35:2) The ambiguous verb form jrpt is translated as a jussive because it is parallel to the jussive form lg}t*.
4tn (35:2) Heb "and let it rejoice, yes [with] rejoicing and shouting." tlyg may be an archaic feminine nominal form (see GKC §130.b).
5tn (35:2) Or, "glory."
6tn (35:2) Or, "glory."
7tn (35:3) Heb "staggering knees."
8tn (35:4) Heb "Say to the hasty of heart," i.e., those whose hearts beat quickly from fear.
9tn (35:4) The jussive form <k#u&v^y{w+, which is subordinated to the preceding imperfect with vav conjunctive, indicates purpose.
10tn (35:6) Heb "burst forth."
11tn (35:6) Or, "Arabah."
12tc (35:8) The Hebrew text reads literally, "and there will be there a road and a way, and the Way of Holiness it will be called." irdw, "and a/the way" is accidentally duplicated; 1Qa does not reflect the repetition of the phrase.
13tn (35:8) The precise meaning of this line is uncertain. The text reads literally, "and it is for them, the one who walks [on the] way." In this context those authorized to use the Way of Holiness would be morally upright people who are the recipients of God's deliverance, in contrast to the morally impure and foolish who are excluded from the new covenant community.
14tn (35:8) In this context "fools" are those who are morally corrupt, not those with limited intellectual capacity.
15tn (35:9) Heb "will go up on it."
16tn (35:10) Heb "and the redeemed will walk, the ransomed of the LORD will return."
17tn (35:10) Heb "[will be] on their head[s]." "Joy" may be likened here to a crown (cf. 2 Sam 1:10). The statement may also be an ironic twist on the idiom "earth/dust on the head" (cf. 2 Sam 1:2; 13:19; 15:32; Job 2:12), referring to a mourning practice.
18tn (35:10) Heb "overtake."
19tn (35:10) Heb "grief and groaning will flee."
1sn (36:2) For a discussion of this title see Cogan and Tadmor, II Kings, 229-30.
2tn (36:2) Heb "he"; the referent (the chief adviser) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
3tn (36:2) Heb "the field of the washer."
4tn (36:4) Heb "What is this object of trust in which you are trusting?"
5tn (36:5) Heb "you say only a word of lips, counsel and might for battle." Sennacherib's message appears to be in broken Hebrew at this point. The phrase "word of lips" refers to mere or empty talk in Prov 14:23.
6tn (36:9) Heb "How can you turn back the face of an official [from among] the least of my master's servants and trust in Egypt for chariots and horsemen?" In vv. 8-9 the chief adviser develops further the argument begun in v. 6. His reasoning seems to be as follows: "In your weakened condition you obviously need military strength. Agree to the king's terms and I will personally give you more horses than you are capable of outfitting. If I, a mere minor official, am capable of giving you such military might, just think what power the king has. There is no way the Egyptians can match our strength. It makes much better sense to deal with us."
7sn (36:10) In v. 10 the chief adviser develops further the argument begun in v. 7. He claims that Hezekiah has offended the LORD and that the LORD has commissioned Assyria as his instrument of discipline and judgment.
8sn (36:11) Aramaic was the diplomatic language of the Assyrian empire.
9tn (36:11) Or, "Hebrew."
10tn (36:12) Heb "To your master and to you did my master send me to speak these words?" The rhetorical question expects a negative answer.
11tn (36:12) Heb "[Is it] not [also] to the men...?" The rhetorical question expects the answer, "Yes, it is."
sn (36:12) The chief adviser alludes to the horrible reality of siege warfare, when the starving people in the besieged city would resort to eating and drinking anything to stay alive.
12tn (36:13) The Hebrew text adds, "and he said."
13tn (36:16) Heb "make with me a blessing and come out to me."
14tn (36:18) Heb "Have the gods of the nations rescued, each his land, from the hand of the king of Assyria?" The rhetorical question expects the answer, "Of course not!"
15tn (36:19) The rhetorical questions in v. 34a suggest the answer, "Nowhere, they seem to have disappeared in the face of Assyria's might."
16tn (36:19) Heb "that they rescued Samaria from my hand?" But this gives the impression that the gods of Sepharvaim were responsible for protecting Samaria, which is obviously not the case. The implied subject of the plural verb "rescued" must be the generic "gods of the nations/lands" (vv. 18, 20).
17tn (36:20) Heb "that the LORD might rescue Jerusalem from my hand?" The logic runs as follows: Since no god has ever been able to withstand the Assyrian onslaught, how can the people of Jerusalem possibly think the LORD will rescue them?
1sn (36:22) As a sign of grief and mourning.
2tn (37:2) Heb "elders of the priests."
3tn (37:3) In the Hebrew text this verse begins with "they said to him."
4tn (37:3) Or, "rebuke," "correction."
5tn (37:3) Or, "contempt."
6tn (37:3) Heb "when sons come to the cervical opening and there is no strength to give birth."
7tn (37:4) Heb "all the words of the chief adviser whom his master, the king of Assyria, sent to taunt the living God."
8tn (37:4) Heb "and rebuke the words which the LORD your God hears."
9tn (37:4) Heb "and lift up a prayer on behalf of the remnant that is found."
10tn (37:6) Heb "by which the servants of the king of Assyria have insulted me."
11tn (37:7) Heb "I will put in him a spirit." The precise sense of jwr, "spirit," is uncertain in this context. It may refer to a spiritual being who will take control of his mind (see 1 Kgs 22:19), or it could refer to a disposition of concern and fear. In either case the LORD's sovereignty over the king is apparent.
12tn (37:7) Heb "cause him to fall," that is, "kill him."
13tn (37:8) Heb "and the chief adviser returned and he found the king of Assyria fighting against Libnah, for he heard that he had departed from Lachish."
14tn (37:9) Heb "He."
15tn (37:9) Heb "Cush."
16tn (37:9) Heb "heard concerning Tirhakah king of Cush, `He has come out to fight with you.'"
17tn (37:9) The Hebrew text has, "and he heard and he sent," but the parallel in 2 Kgs 19:9 has jlvyw bvyw, "and he returned and he sent," i.e., "he again sent."
18tn (37:11) Heb "Look, you have heard what the kings of Assyria have done to all the lands, annihilating them."
19tn (37:11) Heb "and will you be rescued?" The rhetorical question expects the answer, "No, of course not!"
20tn (37:12) Heb "fathers."
21tn (37:12) Heb "Did the gods of the nations whom my fathers destroyed rescue them--Gozan and Haran, and Rezeph and the sons of Eden who are in Telassar?"
22sn (37:13) Lair was a city located in northeastern Babylon. See Cogan and Tadmor, II Kings, 235.
23tc (37:14) The Hebrew text has the plural, "letters," but the final mem is probably dittographic (note the initial mem on the form that immediately follows). Some Greek and Aramaic witnesses have the singular.
24tn (37:14) In the parallel text in 2 Kgs 19:14 the verb has the plural suffix, "them," but this probably reflects a later harmonization to the preceding textual corruption (of "letter" to "letters").
25sn (37:16) This refers to the cherub images that were above the ark of the covenant.
26tn (37:16) Or "the heavens." The same Hebrew term, <y]m^v* (v*m^y]<), may be translated "heavens" or "sky" depending on the context.
27tn (37:17) Heb "Hear all the words of Sennacherib which he sent to taunt the living God."
28tn (37:18) The Hebrew text here has "all the lands," but the parallel text in 2 Kgs 19:17 has "the nations."
29tn (37:19) Heb "and they put their gods in the fire."
30tn (37:19) Heb "so they destroyed them."
31tn (37:20) The parallel text in 2 Kgs 19:19 reads, "that you, LORD, are the only God."
32tn (37:21) The parallel text in 2 Kgs 19:20 reads, "That which you prayed to me concerning Sennacherib king of Assyria I have heard." The verb "I have heard" does not appear in Isa 37:21, where rva probably has a causal sense, "because."
33tn (37:22) Heb "this is the word which the LORD has spoken about him."
34sn (37:22) Zion (Jerusalem) is pictured here as a young, vulnerable daughter whose purity is being threatened by the would-be Assyrian rapist. The personification hints at the reality which the young girls of the city would face if the Assyrians conquer it.
35sn (37:22) Shaking the head was a mocking gesture of derision.
36tn (37:23) Heb "and lifted your eyes on high?"
37tn (37:23) Heb "the holy one of Israel." This divine title pictures the LORD as the sovereign king who rules over his covenant people and exercises moral authority over them. See the notes at 1:4 and 6:3.
38tn (37:24) The Hebrew term translated "sovereign master" here is yn´d)a& (a&d{n´y).
39tn (37:24) Heb "the height of its extremity."
40tc (37:25) The Hebrew text has simply, "I dug and drank water." But the parallel text in 2 Kgs 19:24 has "foreign waters." <yrz, "foreign," probably accidentally dropped out of the Isaianic text by homoioteleuton. Note that the preceding word, <ym, "water," also ends in mem.
41tn (37:26) Having quoted the Assyrian king's arrogant words in vv. 23-24, the LORD now speaks to the king.
42tn (37:26) Heb "Have you not heard?" The rhetorical question expresses the LORD's amazement that anyone might be ignorant of what he is about to say.
43tn (37:26) Heb "formed."
44tn (37:26) Heb "and it is to cause to crash into heaps of ruins fortified cities." The subject of the third feminine singular verb yht is the implied plan, referred to in the preceding lines with third feminine singular pronominal suffixes.
45tn (37:27) Heb "short of hand."
46tn (37:27) Heb "they are plants in the field and green vegetation." The metaphor emphasizes how short-lived these seemingly powerful cities really were. See Ps 90:5-6; Isa 40:6-8, 24.
47tn (37:27) Heb "[they are] grass on the rooftops." See the preceding note.
48tc (37:27) The Hebrew text has "scorched before the standing grain" (perhaps meaning "before it reaches maturity"), but it is preferable to emend hmq, "standing grain," to <ydq, "east wind" (with the support of 1Q Isaa).
49tc (37:28) Heb "your going out and your coming in." The Hebrew text adds here, "and how you have raged against me." However, this line is probably dittographic (note the beginning of the next line).
50tc (37:29) Heb "and your complacency comes up into my ears." The parallelism is improved if innav, "your complacency," is emended to inwav, "your uproar." See Cogan and Tadmor, II Kings, 237-38.
51sn (37:29) The word-picture has a parallel in Assyrian sculpture. See Cogan and Tadmor, II Kings, 238.
52tn (37:30) At this point the word concerning the king of Assyria (vv. 22-29) ends and the LORD again addresses Hezekiah and the people directly (see v. 21).
53tn (37:30) Heb "and this is your sign." In this case the twa, "sign," is a future reminder of God's intervention designated before the actual intervention takes place. For similar "signs" see Exod 3:12 and Isa 7:14-25.
54sn (37:30) This refers to crops that grew up on their own (that is, without cultivation) from the seed planted in past years.
55tn (37:30) Heb "and in the second year."
56tn (37:30) Heb "in the third year."
57tn (37:30) The four plural imperatival verb forms in v. 30b are used rhetorically. The LORD commands the people to plant, harvest, etc. to emphasize the certainty of restored peace and prosperity. See Waltke-O'Connor, BHS, p. 572.
58tn (37:31) Heb "The remnant of the house of Judah that is left will add roots below and produce fruit above."
59tn (37:32) Heb "the zeal of the LORD of hosts." In this context the LORD's "zeal" refers to his intense devotion to and love for his people which prompts him to protect and restore them.
60tn (37:33) Heb "there."
61tn (37:33) Heb "[with] a shield."
62tn (37:35) Heb "for my sake and for the sake of David my servant."
63tn (37:36) Traditionally, "angel."
64tn (37:36) This refers to the Israelites and/or the rest of the Assyrian army.
65tn (37:36) Heb "look, all of them were dead bodies."
66tn (37:37) Heb "and Sennacherib king of Assyria departed and went and returned and lived in Nineveh."
67sn (37:38) The assassination probably took place in 681 B.C.
68sn (37:38) No such Mesopotamian god is presently known. Perhaps the name is a corruption of Nusku.
69sn (37:38) Extra-biblical sources also mention the assassination of Sennacherib, though they refer to only one assassin. See Cogan and Tadmor, II Kings, 239-40.
1tn (38:1) Heb "was sick to the point of dying."
2tn (38:3) Heb "walked before you." For a helpful discussion of the background and meaning of this Hebrew idiom, see Cogan and Tadmor, II Kings, 254.
3tn (38:3) Heb "and with a complete heart."
4tn (38:3) Heb "and that which is good in your eyes I have done."
5tn (38:3) Heb "wept with great weeping."
6tn (38:4) Heb "and the word of the LORD came to Isaiah, saying."
7tn (38:5) Heb "father."
8tc (38:21) If original to Isaiah 38, vv. 21-22 have obviously been misplaced in the course of the text's transmission. See 2 Kgs 20:7-8. Another possibility is that a scribe, familiar with the 2 Kgs version of the story, has appended vv. 21-22 to the account in Isaiah 38.
9tn (38:7) The words "Isaiah replied" are supplied in the translation for clarification. In the present form of the Hebrew text v. 7 is joined directly to v. 6, but vv. 21-22, if original to Isaiah 38, must be inserted here. See 2 Kgs 20:7-8.
10tn (38:8) Heb "the shadow on the steps which [the sun] had gone down, on the steps of Ahaz, with the sun, back ten steps."
sn (38:8) These steps probably functioned as a type of sundial. See HALOT 614, and Cogan and Tadmor, II Kings, 256.
11tn (38:8) Heb "and the sun returned ten steps on the steps which it had gone down."
12tn (38:10) Or, "said."
13tn (38:10) The precise meaning of the phrase ymy ymdb, "in the [?] of my days," is uncertain. According to HALOT 226, ym!d+ is a hapax legomenon meaning "half." Others derive the form from ym!d~, "quiet, rest, peacefulness."
14tn (38:10) The precise meaning of the verb is uncertain. The Pual of dqp occurs only here and in Exod 38:21, where it appears to mean "passed in review" or "mustered." Perhaps the idea is, "I have been called away for the remainder of my years." To bring out the sense more clearly, one can translate, "I am deprived of the rest of my years."
15tn (38:11) The Hebrew text has hy hy (the abbreviated form of hwhy repeated), but this is probably a corruption of hwhy.
16tc (38:11) The Hebrew text has ldj, which appears to be derived from a verbal root meaning "to cease, refrain." But the form has probably suffered an error of transmission; the original form (attested in a few medieval Hebrew MSS) was likely dlj, "world."
17tn (38:12) According to HALOT 217 the noun rwd is a hapax legomenon meaning "dwelling place," derived from a verbal root meaning "live" (see Ps 84:10, ET). For an interpretation that understands the form as the well-attested noun meaning "generation," see Oswalt, Isaiah, 679, note 4.
18tn (38:12) The verb form appears to be a Niphal from hlg, which normally means "uncovered, revealed" in the Niphal. Because of the following reference to a shepherd's tent, some prefer to emend the form to lgnw, a Niphal from llg, "roll," and translate, "is rolled [or, "folded"] up."
19tn (38:12) Heb "I rolled up, like a weaver, my life."
20sn (38:12) For a discussion of the imagery employed here, see Oswalt, Isaiah, 684.
21tn (38:12) Heb "from day to night you bring me to an end."
22tn (38:13) The verb form in the Hebrew text is a Piel from hwv. There are two homonyms hwv, one meaning in the Piel "level, smooth out," the other "set, place." Neither fits in v. 13. It is likely that the original reading was ytuwv, "I cry out," from the verbal root uwv, which occurs exclusively in the Piel.
23tn (38:13) Heb "from day to night you bring me to an end."
24tn (38:14) Or, "moan."
25tn (38:14) Heb "my eyes become weak, toward the height."
26tn (38:14) The Hebrew term translated "sovereign master" here and in v. 16 is yn´d)a& (a&d{n´y).
27tn (38:14) Heb "stand surety for me." Hezekiah seems to be picturing himself as a debtor who is being exploited; he asks that the LORD might relieve his debt and deliver him from the oppressive creditor.
28tn (38:15) Heb "and he has spoken and he has acted."
29tn (38:15) Heb "because of the bitterness of my soul."
30tn (38:16) The translation offered here is purely speculative. The text as it stands is meaningless and probably corrupt. It reads literally, "O lord, on account of them [the suffix is masculine plural], they live, and to all in them [the suffix is feminine plural], life of my spirit."
31tn (38:16) The prefixed verbal form could be taken as indicative, "you restore my health," but the following imperatival form suggests it be understood as an imperfect of request.
32tn (38:17) Heb "Look, for peace bitterness was to me bitter."
33tc (38:17) The Hebrew text reads, "you loved my soul," but this does not fit syntactically with the following prepositional phrase. tqvj, "you loved," may reflect an aural error; most emend the form to tkcj, "you held back."
34tn (38:17) ylb most often appears as a negation, meaning "without," suggesting the meaning "nothingness, oblivion," here. Some translate "decay" or "destruction."
35tn (38:17) Heb "for you threw behind your back all my sins."
36tn (38:18) Or, "for."
37tn (38:18) The negative particle is understood by ellipsis in this line. See GKC §152.z.
38tn (38:20) The infinitive construct is used here to indicate that an action is imminent. See GKC §114.i, and W-O, 610.
39tn (38:20) Heb "and music [or perhaps, "stringed instruments"] we will play."
40tn (38:20) Heb "all the days of our lives in the house of the LORD."
sn (38:20) Note that vv. 21-22 have been placed between vv. 6-7, where they logically belong. See 2 Kgs 20:7-8.
1tn (39:2) Heb ""was happy with."
2tn (39:2) Heb "there was nothing which Hezekiah did not show them in his house and in all his kingdom."
3tn (39:4) Heb "he"; the referent (Isaiah) has been specified in the translation for clarity.
4tn (39:6) Heb "fathers."
5tn (39:7) Heb "Some of your sons, who go out from you, whom you father."
6tn (39:8) Heb "good."
7tn (39:8) Heb "and he said." The verb rma, "say," is sometimes used of what one thinks (that is, says to oneself).
8tn (39:8) Or, "surely."
1tn (40:1) The pronominal suffix is second masculine plural. The identity of the addressee is uncertain: (1) God's people may be addressed, or (2) the identified heralds commanded to comfort Jerusalem.
2tn (40:2) Heb "speak to the heart of Jerusalem." Jerusalem is personified as a woman.
3tn (40:2) Heb "that she is filled [with] her warfare." Some understand abx, "warfare," as meaning "hard service" or "compulsory labor" in this context.
4tn (40:2) Heb "that her punishment is accepted [as satisfactory]."
5tn (40:2) Heb "for she has received from the hand of the LORD double." The principle of the double portion in punishment is also seen in Jer 16:18; 17:18 and Rev 18:6. For examples of the double portion in Israelite law, see Exod 22:4, 7, 9 (double restitution by a thief) and Deut 21:17 (double inheritance portion for the firstborn).
6tn (40:5) Or, "glory." The LORD's "glory" is his theophanic radiance and royal splendor (see Isa 6:3; 24:23; 35:2; 60:1; 66:18-19).
7tn (40:5) Heb "flesh."
8tn (40:5) Or, "indeed."
9tn (40:5) Heb "the mouth of the LORD has spoken."
10tn (40:6) Heb "and he says." Apparently a second "voice" responds to the command of the first "voice."
11tn (40:6) The words "the first voice responds" are supplied in the translation for clarification. The first voice tells the second one what to declare.
12tn (40:6) Heb "all flesh is grass." The point of the metaphor is explained in v. 7.
13tn (40:6) Heb "and all his loyalty." The antecedent of the third masculine suffix is rcb, "flesh," which refers collectively to mankind. The LXX, apparently understanding the antecedent as "grass," reads "glory," but dsj rarely, if ever, has this nuance. The normal meaning of dsj, "faithfulness, loyalty, devotion," fits very well in the argument. Human beings and their faithfulness (verbal expressions of faithfulness are specifically in view) are short-lived and unreliable, in stark contrast to the decrees and promises of the eternal God.
14tn (40:7) The Hebrew text has hwhy jwr, which in this context probably does not refer to the LORD's personal Spirit. The phrase is better translated, "the breath of the LORD," or "the wind of [i.e., sent by] the LORD." The LORD's sovereign control over nature, including the hot desert winds that dry up vegetation, is in view here (cf. Ps 147:18; Isa 59:19).
15tn (40:7) Heb "the people."
16tn (40:8) Heb "but the word of our God stands forever." In this context the divine "word" is his decreed promise assuring Jerusalem that her suffering is over and his glorious return imminent (vv. 1-5).
17tn (40:9) The second feminine singular imperatives are addressed to personified Zion/Jerusalem, who is here told to ascend a high hill and proclaim the good news of the LORD's return to the other towns of Judah. Isa 41:27 and 52:7 speak of a herald sent to Zion, but the masculine singular form rcbm is used in these verses, in contrast to the feminine singular form trcbm employed in 40:9, where Zion is addressed as a herald.
18tn (40:10) Heb "comes as a strong one." The preposition -b= here carries the nuance "in the capacity of." It indicates that the LORD possesses the quality expressed by the noun. See GKC §119.i, and HALOT 104.
19tn (40:10) Heb "his arm rules for him." The LORD's "arm" symbolizes his military power (see Isa 51:9-10; 63:5).
20tn (40:10) As the LORD returns to Jerusalem as a victorious warrior, he brings with him the spoils of victory, called here his "reward" and "prize." These terms might also be translated "wages" and "recompense." Verse 11 indicates that his rescued people, likened to a flock of sheep, are his reward.
21tn (40:12) 1QIsa has <y ym, "waters of the sea."
22tn (40:12) Heb "with a span." A "span" was the distance between the ends of the thumb and the little finger of the spread hand" (BDB 285).
23tn (40:12) Or "the heavens." The same Hebrew term, <y]m^v* (v*m^y]<), may be translated "heavens" or "sky" depending on the context.
24tn (40:12) Heb "or weighed by a third part [of a measure]."
25sn (40:12) The implied answer to the rhetorical questions of v. 12 is "no one but the LORD. The LORD, and no other, created the world. Like a merchant weighing out silver or commodities on a scale, the LORD established the various components of the physical universe in precise proportions.
26tn (40:13) Perhaps the verb is used metonymically here in the sense of "advises" (note the following line).
27tn (40:13) In this context jwr likely refers to the LORD's "mind," or mental faculties, rather than his personal Spirit (see BDB 925).
28tn (40:13) Heb "or [as] the man of his counsel causes him to know?"
29tn (40:14) Heb "With whom did he consult, so that he gave discernment to him?"
30tn (40:14) Heb "and taught him." The vav consecutive with prefixed verbal form continues the previous line. The translation employs an interrogative pronoun for stylistic reasons.
31tn (40:14) The phrase fpvm jra could be translated, "path of justice," but in this context, where creative ability and skill is in view, the phrase is better understood in the sense of "the way that is proper or fitting" (see BDB 1049).
32tn (40:14) Heb "or the way of understanding causes him to know?"
sn (40:14) The implied answer to the rhetorical questions in vv. 13-14 is, "No one." In contrast to Marduk, the creator-god of Mesopotamian myths who receives help from the god of wisdom, the LORD neither needs nor receives any such advice or help. See R. Whybray, Heavenly Counsellor, 64-77.
33tn (40:15) Or, "weighs."
34tn (40:15) Or, "islands."
35tn (40:16) The words "for a sacrifice" are supplied in the translation for clarification.
36sn (40:16) The point is that not even the Lebanon forest could supply enough wood and animals for an adequate sacrifice to the LORD.
37tn (40:17) Heb "[as derived] from nothing and unformed."
38tn (40:19) Heb "pours out."
39tn (40:20) The first two words of the verse (hmwrt /ksmh) are problematic. Some take /ksm as an otherwise unattested Pual participle from /ks, "be poor," and translate "the one who is impoverished." hmwrt, "contribution," can then be taken as an adverbial accusative, "with respect to a contribution," and the entire line translated, "the one who is too impoverished for such a contribution [i.e., the metal idol of v. 19?] selects wood that will not rot." However, /ksm is probably the name of a tree used in idol manufacturing (cognate with Akkadian musukkanu, cf. H. Cohen, Hapax Legomena, 133). /ksm may be a scribal interpretive addition attempting to specify Ju or Ju may be a scribal attempt to categorize /ksm. How an idol constitutes a hmwrt, "contribution," is not entirely clear.
40tn (40:20) Or, "set up."
41tn (40:22) Heb "the circle of the earth."
42tn (40:22) The words "before him" are supplied in the translation for clarification.
43tn (40:22) The otherwise unattested noun qd, translated here "thin curtain," is apparently derived from the verbal root qqd, "crush," from which is derived the adjective qD~, "thin." (See HALOT 229.) The nuance "curtain" is implied from the parallelism (see "tent" in the next line).
44tn (40:22) The meaning of the otherwise unattested verb jtm, "spread out," is determined from the parallelism (note the corresponding verb "stretch out" in the previous line) and supported by later Hebrew and Aramaic cognates. See HALOT 654.
45tn (40:22) Heb "like a tent [in which] to live."
46tn (40:25) Heb "Holy One." See the notes at 1:4 and 6:3.
47tn (40:26) Heb "Lift on high your eyes and see."
48tn (40:26) The words "heavenly lights" are supplied in the translation for clarification. See the following lines.
49tn (40:26) Heb "the one who brings out by number their host." The stars are here likened to a huge army that the LORD leads out. Perhaps the next line pictures God calling roll. If so, the final line may be indicating that none of them dares "go AWOL." ("AWOL" is a military acronym for "absent without leave.")
50tn (40:27) Heb "my way is hidden from the LORD."
51tn (40:27) Heb "and from my God my justice passes away."
52tn (40:28) Heb "the ends of the earth," but this is a merism, where the earth's extremities stand for its entirety, i.e., the extremities and everything in between them.
53sn (40:28) Exiled Israel's complaint (v. 27) implies that God might be limited in some way. Perhaps he, like so many of the pagan gods, has died. Or perhaps his jurisdiction is limited to Judah and does not include Babylon. Maybe he is unable to devise an adequate plan to rescue his people, or is unable to execute it. But v. 28 affirms that he is not limited temporally or spatially nor is his power and wisdom restricted in any way. He can and will deliver his people, if they respond in hopeful faith (v. 31a).
54tn (40:30) Heb "stumbling they stumble." The verbal idea is emphasized by the infinitive absolute.
55tn (40:31) The words "for the LORD's help" are supplied in the translation for clarification.
56tn (40:31) Heb "they rise up [on] wings like eagles."
1tn (41:1) Or, "islands."
2tn (41:1) The Hebrew term fpvm could be translated "judgment," but here it seems to refer to the dispute or debate between the LORD and the nations.
3sn (41:2) The expression this one from the east refers to the Persian conqueror Cyrus, as later texts indicate (see 44:28-45:6; 46:11; 48:14-16).
4tn (41:2) The interrogative particle is understood by ellipsis.
5tn (41:2) Heb "[in] righteousness called him to his foot."
6tn (41:2) Heb "he [the LORD] places before him [Cyrus] nations."
7tn (41:2) The verb D+r+y~ is an otherwise unattested Hiphil form from hdr, "rule." But the Hiphil makes no sense with "kings" as object; one must understand an ellipsis and supply "him" (Cyrus) as the object. 1QIsa has drwy, which appears to be a Hiphil form from dry, "go down." Others suggest reading dr)y´, a Qal form from ddr, "beat down."
8sn (41:2) The point is that they are powerless before Cyrus's military power and scatter before him.
9tn (41:3) Heb "[in] peace."
10tn (41:3) The text reads literally, "a way with his feet he does not come [or, "enter"]." One could translate, "by a way he was not [previously] entering with his feet." This would mean that he is advancing into new territory and expanding his conquests. The translation above assumes this is a hyperbolic description to his speedy advance. He moves so quickly he does enter the way with his feet, i.e., his feet don't even touch the ground. See C. R. North, Second Isaiah, 94.
11tn (41:4) Heb "Who acts and accomplishes?"
12tn (41:4) The interrogative particle is understood by ellipsis (note the preceding line).
13tn (41:4) Heb "I, the LORD, [am with] the first, and with the last ones I [am] he."
14tn (41:5) Or, "islands."
15tn (41:5) Heb "the ends of the earth," but this is a merism, where the earth's extremities stand for its entirety, i.e., the extremities and everything in between them.
16tn (41:6) Heb "each his neighbor helps."
17tn (41:7) The verb "encourages" is understood by ellipsis (note the preceding line).
18tn (41:7) Heb "saying of the welding, `It is good.'"
19tn (41:8) Or perhaps, "covenantal partner" (see 1 Kgs 5:15 HT = 5:1 ET; 2 Chr 20:7).
20tn (41:9) Heb "whom I have taken hold of [i.e., to lead back]."
21tn (41:10) According to BDB (1043), the verb uT*v=T! in the second line of the poetic couplet is a Hitpael form from the root huv, "gaze" (with metathesis of the stem prefix and the first root letter). Taking the Hitpael as iterative, one may then translate, "do not anxiously look about." However, the alleged Hitpael form of huv only occurs here and in verse 23. The KB supplement (192) proposes that the verb is instead a Qal form from the root utv, "fear," which is attested in cognate Semitic languages, including Ugaritic (discovered after the publishing of BDB), suggests the existence of this root. The poetic structure of v. 10 also supports the proposal, for the form in question is in synonymous parallelism to ary, "fear."
22tn (41:10) The "right hand" is a symbol of the LORD's power to deliver (Exod 15:6, 12) and protect (Ps 63:9 HT = v. 8, ET). Here qdx has its well-attested nuance of "vindicated righteousness," i.e., "victory, deliverance" (see 45:8; 51:5, and BDB 841-42).
23tn (41:11) Heb "the men of your strife."
24tn (41:11) Heb "like nothing."
25tn (41:12) Heb "the men of your struggle."
26tn (41:12) Heb "the men of your battle."
27tn (41:14) Heb "O worm, Jacob." The worm metaphor suggests that Jacob is insignificant and despised.
28tn (41:14) On the basis of the parallelism (note "worm") and an alleged Akkadian cognate, some read "louse" or "weevil."
29tn (41:14) Heb "your kinsman redeemer." A lag, "kinsman redeemer," was a protector of the extended family's interests.
30tn (41:14) Heb "the Holy One of Israel." See the note at 1:4.
31tn (41:15) Heb "into."
32tn (41:15) Heb "owner of two-mouths," i.e., double-edged.
33sn (41:15) The mountains and hills symbolize hostile nations that are obstacles to Israel's restoration.
34tn (41:16) Heb "the Holy One of Israel." See the note at 1:4.
35tn (41:17) Heb "will answer them."
36tn (41:20) The words "I will do this" are supplied in the translation for clarification. The Hebrew text has here simply, "in order that."
37tn (41:20) Heb "they."
38tn (41:20) Heb "hand."
39tn (41:20) Heb "the Holy One of Israel." See the note at 1:4.
40tn (41:20) Or, "created it."
41tn (41:21) Heb "strong [words]," see HALOT 870.
42sn (41:21) Apparently this challenge is addressed to the pagan idol gods, see vv. 23-24.
43tn (41:22) Heb "As for the former things, tell us what they are!"
44tn (41:22) Heb "so we might set [them to] our heart."
45tn (41:22) Heb "and might know their outcome."
46tn (41:23) Heb "Declare the coming things, with respect to the end."
47tc (41:23) The translation assumes the Qere (harnw, from ary, "be afraid").
tn (41:23) Heb "so we might be frightened and afraid together." On the meaning of the verb utv, see the note at v. 10.
48tn (41:24) Heb "an object of disgust [is he who] chooses you."
49sn (41:25) That is, Cyrus the Persian. See the note at v. 2.
50tn (41:25) Heb "[one] from the rising of the sun [who] calls in my name."
51tn (41:25) The Hebrew text has abyw, "and he comes," but this is likely a corruption of an original sbyw, from swb, "step on."
52tn (41:26) The words "who announced it" are supplied in the translation for clarification. The interrogative particle and verb are understood by ellipsis (see the preceding line).
53tn (41:27) The Hebrew text reads simply, "First to Zion, `Look here they are!'" The words "I decreed" are supplied in the translation for clarification.
54tc (41:29) The Hebrew text has /wa, "deception" (i.e., "false"), but 1QIsa has /ya, "nothing," which forms a better parallel with spa, "nothing," in the next line. See also 40:17 and 41:12.
55tn (41:29) Heb "their statues are wind and nothing."
1sn (42:1) Verses 1-7 contain the first of Isaiah's "servant songs," which describe the ministry of a special, ideal servant who accomplishes God's purposes for Israel and the nations. This song depicts the servant as a just king who brings justice to the earth and relief for the oppressed. The other songs appear in 49:1-13; 50:4-11; and 52:13-53:12.
2tn (42:1) Heb "he will bring out justice."
3sn (42:1) Like the ideal king portrayed in Isa 11:1-9, the servant is energized by the divine spirit and establishes justice on the earth.
4tn (42:2) Heb "he will not cause his voice to be heard in the street."
5sn (42:3) The "crushed reed" and "dim wick" symbolize the weak and oppressed who are on the verge of extinction.
6tn (42:3) Heb "faithfully he will bring out justice."
7tn (42:4) For rhetorical effect the terms used to describe the "crushed (Jxr) reed" and "dim (hhk) wick" in v. 3 are repeated here.
8tn (42:4) Or, "islands."
9tn (42:4) Or, "his law," or "his instruction."
10tn (42:5) Heb "the God." The definite article here indicates distinctiveness or uniqueness.
11tn (42:5) Heb "and its offspring."
12tn (42:5) Heb "and spirit [i.e., "breath"] to the ones walking in it."
13tn (42:6) Heb "call you in righteousness." The pronoun "you" is masculine singular, referring to the servant. See the note at 41:2.
14tn (42:6) The translation assumes the verb is derived from the root rxn, "protect." Some prefer to derive it from the root rxy, "form."
15tn (42:6) Heb "a covenant of people." A person cannot literally be a covenant; tyrb is probably metonymic here, indicating a covenant mediator. The precise identity of <u, "people," is uncertain. In v. 5 <u refers to mankind, and the following reference to "nations" also favors this. But in 49:8, where the phrase <u tyrb occurs again, Israel seems to be in view.
16sn (42:6) Light here symbolizes deliverance from bondage and oppression; note the parallelism in 49:6b and in 51:4-6..
17sn (42:7) This does not refer to literal physical healing of the blind. As the next two lines suggest, this refers metonymically to freeing captives from their dark prisons where their eyes have grown unaccustomed to light.
18sn (42:7) This does not refer to hardened, dangerous criminals, who would have been executed for their crimes in ancient Near Eastern society. This verse refers to political prisoners or victims of social injustice.
19tn (42:9) Heb "the former things, look, they have come."
20tn (42:9) Heb "before they sprout up, I cause you to hear." The pronoun "you" is plural, referring to the people of Israel. In this verse "the former things" are the LORD's earlier predictive oracles which have come to pass, while "the new things" are predicted events that have not yet begun to take place. "The former things" are earlier events in Israel's history which God announced beforehand, such as the Exodus (see 43:16-18). "The new things" are the predictions about the servant (42:1-7). and may also include Cyrus's conquests (41:25-27).
21tn (42:10) Heb "his praise." The phrase stands parallel to "new song" in the previous line.
22tn (42:10) Heb "and its fullness."
23tn (42:10) Or, "islands."
24tn (42:12) Heb "Let them ascribe to the LORD glory."
25tn (42:12) Heb "and his praise in the coastlands [or, "islands"] let them declare."
26tn (42:13) Heb "like a man of war he stirs up zeal."
27tn (42:13) Or, perhaps, "he triumphs over his enemies."
28tn (42:14) Heb "silent."
29sn (42:14) The imagery depicts the LORD as a warrior who is eager to fight and can no longer hold himself back from the attack.
30tn (42:15) Heb "I will dry up the mountains and hills." The "mountains and hills" stand by synecdoche for the trees that grow on them. Some prefer to derive the verb from a homonymic root and translate, "I will lay waste."
31tc (42:15) As it stands, the Hebrew text reads, "I will turn streams into coastlands [or, "islands"], but this makes little sense. The translation assumes an emendation of <yya to twyx, "dry places."
32sn (42:15) The imagery of this verse, which depicts the LORD bringing a curse of infertility to the earth, metaphorically describes how the LORD will destroy his enemies.
33tn (42:16) Heb "a way they do not know."
34tn (42:16) Heb "in paths they do not know I will make them walk."
35tn (42:16) Heb "and the rough ground into a level place."
36tn (42:17) Heb "be ashamed with shame."
37tn (42:18) Heb "look to see."
38tc (42:19) The precise meaning of <lvm in this context is uncertain. In later biblical Hebrew the form (which appears to be a Pual participle from the root <lv) occurs as a proper name, Meshullam. The Pual of <lv, "be complete," is attested with the meaning "repaid, requited," but that makes little sense here. BDB 1023 relates the form to the denominative verb <lv, "be at peace," and paraphrases, "one in a covenant of peace." Some emend the form to <l*v=m), "their ruler," or to yj!L*v%m=, "my sent [or, "commissioned"] one," which fits nicely in the parallelism (note "my messenger" in the previous line). The translation above assumes an emendation to ym!l=v) omK=, "like my ally." (Isaiah uses wmk in 30:22 and perhaps 51:5; for yml=v), "my ally," see Ps 7:5 HT = v. 4 ET.)
39tn (42:19) Heb "Who is blind but my servant, and deaf like my messenger I send? Who is blind like my commissioned one, blind like the servant of the LORD?" The point of the rhetorical questions is that no one is as blind/deaf as this servant. In this context the LORD's "servant" is exiled Israel (cf. 41:8-9), which is spiritually blind and deaf and has failed to fulfill God's purpose for it. This servant stands in contrast to the ideal "Israel" of the servant songs.
40tn (42:20) The consonantal text (Kethib) has a perfect, 2ms; the marginal reading (Qere) has an infinitive absolute, which functions here as a finite verb.
41tn (42:20) Heb "but you do not guard [i.e., retain in your memory]."
42tn (42:21) Heb "The LORD was pleased for the sake of his righteousness [or, "justice"], he was magnifying [the] law and was making [it] glorious." The LORD contrasts his good intentions for the people with their present crisis (v. 22). To demonstrate his just character and attract the nations, the LORD wanted to showcase his law among and through Israel (Deut 4:5-8). But Israel disobeyed (v. 24) and failed to carry out their commission.
43tc (42:22) The Hebrew text has <yr]WjB^, "young men," but the text should be emended to <yrohB=, "in holes."
44tn (42:22) Heb "and made to be hidden."
45tn (42:22) Heb "they became loot and there was no one rescuing, plunder and there was no one saying, `Bring back'."
46tn (42:23) The interrogative particle is understood in the second line by ellipsis (note the preceding line).
47tn (42:24) Heb "Who gave to the robber Jacob, and Israel to the looters?" In the first line the consonantal text (Kethib) has hswvm, a Polel participle from hsv, "plunder." The marginal reading (Qere) is hsyvm, a noun meaning "plunder." In this case one could translate, "Who handed Jacob over as plunder?"
48tn (42:24) Heb "they were not willing in his ways to walk, and they did not listen to his law."
49tn (42:25) Heb "strength."
50tn (42:25) Heb "and it blazed against him all around, but he did not know." The subject of the third feminine singular verb "blazed" is the divine hmj, "anger," mentioned in the previous line.
51tn (42:25) Heb "and it burned against him, but he did not set [it] upon [the] heart."
1tn (43:1) Or, "redeem." See the note at 41:14.
2tn (43:2) The verb is understood by ellipsis (note the preceding line).
3tn (43:2) Heb "burn."
4tn (43:3) Heb "the Holy One of Israel." See the note at 1:4.
5tn (43:4) Heb "Since you are precious in my eyes and you are honored."
6tn (43:7) Heb "everyone who is called by my name."
7tn (43:9) Heb "and the former things was causing us to hear?"
8tn (43:10) Or, "know."
9tn (43:10) Heb "and after me, there will not be."
10tn (43:13) Heb "hand."
11tn (43:14) Or, "kinsman redeemer." See the note at 41:14.
12tn (43:14) Heb "the Holy One of Israel." See the note at 1:4.
13tn (43:14) Heb "and I bring down [as] fugitives all of them."
14tn (43:14) The Hebrew text reads literally, "as for the Babylonians, in ships their joyful shout." This might be paraphrased, "even the Babylonians in the ships [over which] they joyfully shouted." The point would be that the LORD caused the Babylonians to flee for safety in the ships in which they took such great pride. A slight change in vocalization yields the reading "into mourning songs," which provides a good contrast with "joyful shout." The prefixed bet would indicate identity.
15tn (43:15) Heb "your Holy One." See the note at 1:4.
16tn (43:17) Heb "led out chariots and horses." The words "to destruction" are supplied in the translation for clarification. The verse refers to the destruction of the Egyptians at the Red Sea.
17tn (43:17) Heb "lay down."
18tn (43:18) Heb "the former things."
19tn (43:19) Heb "sprouts up."
20tn (43:19) Or, "know."
21tn (43:19) The Hebrew texts has "streams," probably under the influence of v. 20. 1QIsa has twbytn, "paths."
22tn (43:21) Heb "[so] they might declare my praise."
23tn (43:22) Or, "strive."
24tn (43:23) Heb "with." The words "by demanding" are supplied in the translation for clarification.
25tn (43:24) That is, calamus.
26tn (43:24) Heb "you did not saturate me."
27sn (43:24) In vv. 22-24 the LORD appears to be condemning his people for failure to bring the proper sacrifices. However, this is problematic. If this refers to the nation's behavior while in exile, such cultic service was impossible and could hardly be expected by the LORD. If this refers to the nation's conduct before the exile, it contradicts other passages that depict Israel as bringing excessive sacrifices (see, e.g., Isa 1:11-14; Jer 6:20; Amos 4:4-5, 5:21-23). Rather than being a condemnation of Israel's failure to bring sacrifices, these verses are better taken as a highly rhetorical comment on the worthlessness of Israel's religious ritual. They may have brought sacrifices, but not to the LORD, for he did not accept them or even want them. See C. R. North, The Second Isaiah, 127, and R. Whybray, Isaiah 40-66, 91.
28tn (43:26) Heb "you, tell in order that you may be right."
29tn (43:27) Heb "your first father." This could refer to Abraham (see 51:2), but elsewhere in Isaiah he does not appear in a negative light (see 29:22; 41:8; 63:16). A more likely candidate is Jacob/Israel, also referred to as the nation's "father" elsewhere (see 58:14; 63:16).
30tn (43:27) On the meaning of the term Jylm, see HALOT 590. This may refer to the nation's prophets, priests, and/or kings.
31tn (43:28) The word "subjected" is supplied in the translation for stylistic reasons.
1sn (44:2) Jeshurun is a poetic name for Israel; it occurs here and in Deut 32:15; 33:5, 26.
2tn (44:3) Heb "the thirsty." Parallelism suggests that dry ground is in view (see "dry land" in the next line.)
3tn (44:3) Heb "and streams."
4tn (44:4) The Hebrew term /yb is usually taken as a preposition, in which case one might translate, "among the grass." But /yb is probably the name of a tree (cf. North, Second Isaiah, 133). If one alters the preposition bet to kaf, one can then read, "like a binu-tree." (1QIsa supports this reading.) This forms a nice parallel to "like poplars" in the next line. ryxj is functioning as an adverbial accusative of location.
5tn (44:5) The Hebrew text has a Qal verb form, "and another will call by the name of Jacob." With support from Symmachus (an ancient Greek textual witness), some read the Niphal, "and another will be called by the name of Jacob."
6tn (44:5) Heb "and by the name of Israel he will title." Some, with support from several ancient versions, prefer to change the Piel (active) verb form to a Pual (passive), "and he will be titled by the name of Israel."
7tn (44:6) Heb "his kinsman redeemer." See the note at 41:14.
8tn (44:6) Traditionally, "the LORD of hosts." See the note at 1:9.
9tn (44:7) Heb "let him call (or "proclaim")."
10tc (44:7) The Hebrew text reads, "from (the time) I established an ancient people, and the coming things." Various emendations have been proposed; the present translation assumes the reading: twyta <lwum <yuymvm, literally, "the ones causing to hear from antiquity coming things."
11tn (44:7) Heb and those things which are coming let them declare for themselves."
12tn (44:8) BDB 923 derives this verb from an otherwise unattested root hjr, while HALOT 403 lists it under hry, which they define as "be stupefied" on the basis of an Arabic cognate. The form is likely a corruption of waryt, the reading attested in 1QIsa.
13tn (44:8) Heb "rock" or "rocky cliff," a title that depicts God as a protective refuge in his role as sovereign king.
14tn (44:10) The rhetorical question is sarcastic. The sense is, "Who is foolish enough...?"
15tn (44:11) The pronoun "his" probably refers to the one who forms/casts an idol (v. 10), in which case it refers to the craftsman's associates in the idol-manufacturing guild.
16sn (44:11) The point seems to be this: If the idols are the mere products of human hands, then those who trust in them will be disappointed, for man-made gods are incapable of helping their "creators."
17tn (44:12) The noun dxum, which refers to some type of tool used for cutting, occurs only here and in Jer 10:3. See HALOT 615.
18tn (44:12) That is, an idol.
19tn (44:12) Heb "and there is no strength."
20tn (44:13) Heb "stretches out a line."
21tn (44:13) Heb "he makes an outline with the [?]." The noun drc occurs only here; it apparently refers to some type of tool or marker.
22tn (44:13) Heb "works," or "makes."
23tn (44:13) Heb "he makes it like the pattern of a man."
24tn (44:13) Heb "like the glory of man to sit [in] a house."
25tn (44:14) It is not certain what type of tree this otherwise unattested noun refers to.
26tn (44:14) Heb "strengthens for himself," i.e., "secures for himself" (see BDB 55).
27tn (44:14) Some prefer to emend /ra to zra, "cedar," but the otherwise unattested noun appears to have an Akkadian cognate, meaning "cedar." See H. Cohen, Hapax Legomena, 44-45. HALOT 90 offers the meaning "laurel."
28tn (44:15) Heb "and it becomes burning [i.e., firewood] for a man."
29tn (44:15) Or, perhaps, "them."
30tn (44:18) Heb "for their eyes are smeared over so they cannot see, so their heart cannot be wise."
31tn (44:19) There is no formal interrogative sign here, but the context seems to indicate these are rhetorical questions. See GKC §150.a.
32tn (44:20) Or, perhaps, "he eats on an ash heap."
33tn (44:20) Heb "Is it not a lie in my right hand?"
34tc (44:21) The verb in the Hebrew text is a Niphal imperfect with a pronominal suffix. Perhaps one could translate, "you will not be forgotten by me," but the construction is peculiar. The translation above assumes an emendation to a Qal form. See GKC §117.x. "Do not forget me" would make a good parallel with "remember these things" in the first line.
35tn (44:22) Heb "I blot out like a cloud your rebellious deeds, and like a cloud your sins." "Rebellious deeds" and "sins" stand by metonymy for the guilt they produce. Both bu and /nu refer to the clouds in the sky. It is tempting for stylistic purposes to translate the second with "fog" or "mist," but this distinction between the synonyms is unwarranted here. The point of the simile seems to be this: The LORD forgives their sins, causing them to vanish just as clouds disappear from the sky (see Job 7:9; 30:15).
36tn (44:22) Heb "redeem." See the note at 41:14.
37tn (44:23) Heb "acts."
38tn (44:23) Heb "lower regions." This refers to Sheol and forms a merism with "sky" in the previous line. See Pss 63:9 (ET), 71:20.
